  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5692782417314523428">Mary Saunders, a lower-class London schoolgirl, was born into rough cloth but hungered for lace and the trappings of a higher station than her family would ever know. In 18th-century England, Mary's shrewd instincts will get her only so far, and she despairs of the plans made for her to carve out a trade as a seamstress or a maid. Unwilling to bend to such a destiny, Mary </span>
  <span id="freeText5692782417314523428" style="display:none">Mary Saunders, a lower-class London schoolgirl, was born into rough cloth but hungered for lace and the trappings of a higher station than her family would ever know. In 18th-century England, Mary's shrewd instincts will get her only so far, and she despairs of the plans made for her to carve out a trade as a seamstress or a maid. Unwilling to bend to such a destiny, Mary strikes out on a painful, fateful journey all her own. Inspired by the obscure historical figure Mary Saunders, Slammerkin is a provocative, graphic tale and a rich feast of an historical novel. Author Emma Donoghue probes the gap between a young girl's quest for freedom and a better life and the shackles that society imposes on her. "Never give up your liberty."</span>
  <a data-text-id="5692782417314523428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_51038516" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51038516" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/202005-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298608026p2/202005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51038516?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/202005-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/202005-melanie?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51038516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7248961428080067899">I never leave the house without a red ribbon.<br/><br/>Mary Saunders, the focus of Slammerkin, is thrown out<br/>of her house after being raped for her desire for a red ribbon.<br/><br/>Does the red ribbon establish a kinship between Mary and<br/>me? Perhaps. Lacking a common desire or situation, the<br/>reader may have difficulty opening herself to a character<br/>– in my case, the relationship between a middle-aged<br/>librarian and a doomed teenaged prostitute.<br/><br/>Slammerkin places a very young woman in a desperately<br/>poor household, where sh</span>
  <span id="freeText7248961428080067899" style="display:none">I never leave the house without a red ribbon.<br /><br />Mary Saunders, the focus of Slammerkin, is thrown out<br />of her house after being raped for her desire for a red ribbon.<br /><br />Does the red ribbon establish a kinship between Mary and<br />me? Perhaps. Lacking a common desire or situation, the<br />reader may have difficulty opening herself to a character<br />– in my case, the relationship between a middle-aged<br />librarian and a doomed teenaged prostitute.<br /><br />Slammerkin places a very young woman in a desperately<br />poor household, where she is neither loved nor consulted<br />about how her life will unfold.<br /><br />All evidence points to a miserable and colorless<br />continuation of her mother’s life of poverty, drudgery,<br />and subjugation that was sealed when her father<br />was killed in a misguided protest by men who believed that<br />they were going to lose, literally lose, eleven days of their<br />lives when the government changed to the Gregorian<br />calendar in 1752 -that they would lose time.<br /><br />I was fascinated by the subjective inconstancy of Mary’s<br />perception of time. In her mother’s house, time is nearly a<br />solid mass, changing only by suffering and the family’s<br />heartless response to Mary’s pregnancy. This response, a<br />product of the times, is doled out without mercy.<br /><br />How could the family understand the depth of Mary’s need<br />to escape the faded beige of their lives, or the magical hope<br />symbolized by that red ribbon? And yet, how could a mother<br />cast out her raped, pregnant daughter?<br /><br />(As I write, I realize that Mary’s mother is the only truly<br />unforgivable character in the book. Perhaps my modern-time<br />sensibility intrudes. All of the subsequent damage and<br />tragedy that defined Mary’s brief time, and all of the bitter<br />focus on the actual material that she craved in this world,<br />began with this primal betrayal. If she was not loved for<br />what was within, she could, at least, adorn herself with the transitory beauty of clothes.)<br /><br />Time, and the times, were different when Mary fled<br />to London. London was fast-paced, and the woman who<br />accepted her into the sisterhood of prostitutes were fast.<br />Doll’s love and practical guidance showed Mary that society<br />can tolerate – even require – actions and beliefs far larger<br />than she had ever imagined. Through prostitution, Mary<br />acquired financial independence and freedom to see some of<br />thewonders of her modern world. Likethe fireworks over<br />London, she and her sisters of the night were brief flashes<br />of beauty, dressed in their colorful slammerkins (loose<br />dresses) and masked behind their paint.<br /><br />Mary’s sudden need to escape a street thug impelled her<br />to Magdalene Hospital, a residence founded to purge the<br />evil from the street-wise women. Time was suspended there,<br />with silence, blandness, and time to think without fearing<br />starvation or death in the freezing streets. With Doll’s death,<br />Mary realizes that she has to leave London, and her<br />retreat ends in a desperate flight from the sanctuary<br />to the town where her mother had grown up. Glimpses<br />of the possibilities there almost melt her cynicism, but<br />her nature has been formed, and she can not escape.<br /><br />This novel is based, loosely, on the actual life of a Mary<br />Saunders who was executed for murder in 1764. From the<br />beginning of the novel, when Mary is 13, to her death by<br />hanging at age 16, Mary passes through more lifetimes<br />than many experience in ten times the years.<br /><br />How many such lifetimes can a child endure? For Mary is a<br />child, and my working-class perception of childhood<br />makes me ache for this young girl, whose only<br />transgression was the love of a piece of red ribbon.<br /><br />How does the red ribbon bind me to Mary’s life? For both the<br />18th -century child and the 21st century woman, the red<br />ribbon symbolizes hope. Mary’s hope for a better life is<br />destroyed, but the hopes of my Eastern European Jewish<br />ancestors for the children who would be born in the new<br />world, and would escape the Evil Eye of the old. have<br />been realized. After reading Slammerkin, I realize anew<br />that I am, indeed, blessed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7248961428080067899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I’ve learned that she’s won or been nominated for several prizes, and that her historical fiction has tended to be centered around the different ways that in past times women were perceived; the pretty limited choices that women often had; and the real stories of certain women that became rather sensational in their own time – or perhaps</span>
  <span id="freeText3360961653682948914" style="display:none"><br /><b>more knowledge -&gt; altered opinion -&gt; explanation &amp; new rating</b><br /><br />Since I wrote this review almost four years ago, I’ve learned more about the author, Emma Donoghue. I’ve learned that she’s won or been nominated for several prizes, and that her historical fiction has tended to be centered around the different ways that in past times women were perceived; the pretty limited choices that women often had; and the real stories of certain women that became rather sensational in their own time – or perhaps <i>scandalous</i> would be a better word?<br /><br />Knowing these and other bits about Donoghue has caused me to think that I may have been somewhat dismissive of this book, and the author, those four years ago. So (and I’m <i>sure</i> this will <i>thrill</i> Ms. Donoghue) I’m raising my rating for the book to a four. If that knowledge would have kept me from reading it, yes. Otherwise no. I have no idea what the answer is. (<u><i>oh come on, Ted!</i></u>)<br /><br /><b>Why didn't you like the book more?</b> I don't really know. In this particular case it might be because it seemed the book was all over the place. It was in the last hundred pages that it sort of fell apart for me. But I don't really <u><s>care</s> know</u> why. <br /><br /><b>Could it be because you're lacking in the skills of fictional analysis &amp; criticism?</b> Certainly.<br /><br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /></span>

<div id="review_11093674" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11093674" />
    <a title="Francine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/705590-francine"><img alt="Francine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296600423p2/705590.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/11093674?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=brit-lit">brit-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/705590-francine?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11093674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8285774050414074320">I was highly disappointed by this book, especially since it received some really good reviews. The writing style was fine, and Emma Donoghue painted a fairly accurate portrait of 18th century London. (These are the only things which made me give this novel 2 stars...otherwise, it would've been a 1-star book.) I thought the narrative's main flaw lay in its heroine, Mary Saunders. To me, she was very 2-dimensional: she was vain, vapid, egotistical, wholly unapologetic (about her thoughts/feelings/</span>
  <span id="freeText8285774050414074320" style="display:none">I was highly disappointed by this book, especially since it received some really good reviews. The writing style was fine, and Emma Donoghue painted a fairly accurate portrait of 18th century London. (These are the only things which made me give this novel 2 stars...otherwise, it would've been a 1-star book.) I thought the narrative's main flaw lay in its heroine, Mary Saunders. To me, she was very 2-dimensional: she was vain, vapid, egotistical, wholly unapologetic (about her thoughts/feelings/actions) that she was just completely unrelatable. I found her to be very unsympathetic, and for me, that made getting through the book very difficult. Many times, as readers, we look for something within a character which can be relatable to our own lives, but the characterization of Mary Saunders was such that I just found her intolerable and insufferable. Even in the 3rd part of the novel, when she seemingly wants to try to change her ways, she still comes off as disingenuous. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8285774050414074320" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_13938592" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13938592" />
    <a title="Phil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/797114-phil"><img alt="Phil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200618225p2/797114.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/13938592?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Phil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Phil" href="/user/show/797114-phil">Phil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Historical fiction fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer13938592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14713186282803015427">This book is anti-erotica. Its about a girl who is forced into prostitution by a totally heartless world (London in the eighteeth century) and who progresses in the course of the book from an innocent to the most depraved of humanity. The story wends its way from beginning to end and is interesting and readable, but it is a crabbed view of humanity, unlightened by any hope of redemption or joy. I enjoyed reading it once but its not one of those books I will seek out again.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8967820910028445165">It's strange that when you don't like a book you can come up with a lot of reasons why, yet when I love one I just say "Fantastic. Read it." This book had a lot of ingredients for me to love, but it just fell completely flat. I felt absolutely nothing for the main character, Mary. I think that was the crux of the problem. She is just a psychopath. I mean at least give me a better reason for her to willingly give into that ribbon peddler at age 14 than she just likes colorful hair bows. What? Com</span>
  <span id="freeText8967820910028445165" style="display:none">It's strange that when you don't like a book you can come up with a lot of reasons why, yet when I love one I just say "Fantastic. Read it." This book had a lot of ingredients for me to love, but it just fell completely flat. I felt absolutely nothing for the main character, Mary. I think that was the crux of the problem. She is just a psychopath. I mean at least give me a better reason for her to willingly give into that ribbon peddler at age 14 than she just likes colorful hair bows. What? Come on. Skip this one and read Room by Donoghue instead.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer5336380" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11117499866905148272">Most depressing book EVER! Considering that I am currently reading Everything Is Illuminated and We Wish to Inform You That Tomorrow We Will Be Killed With Our Families right now, and I picked this book up because I apparently was an idiot and thought it would be relief from those two books … well, consider my idiocy, please. Not a wise move on my part.<br/><br/>Depressing, but amazingly written. One of the best books I’ve read in a long while. As dreadful as some of the characters are, they are all engag</span>
  <span id="freeText11117499866905148272" style="display:none">Most depressing book EVER! Considering that I am currently reading Everything Is Illuminated and We Wish to Inform You That Tomorrow We Will Be Killed With Our Families right now, and I picked this book up because I apparently was an idiot and thought it would be relief from those two books … well, consider my idiocy, please. Not a wise move on my part.<br /><br />Depressing, but amazingly written. One of the best books I’ve read in a long while. As dreadful as some of the characters are, they are all engaging. Beautiful array of female characters, who all show different aspects of being a woman in 18th-century society. You could definitely tell that the author had done her research, from everything to clothing to food to society to architecture. Considering the research fail I usually encounter, this book was one long session of me saying YES!<br /><br />I wanted, wanted, wanted Mary to have a happy ending, to reform herself and marry Daffy, but she didn’t. She failed pretty spectacularly at the whole reform thing, though I wonder if she were even capable of it. Or even if she would have been successful at being a wife, considering she was barren, though I imagine that Daffy would have grown to accept that after a time. This book is just a slow ebbing of Mary’s innocence. You think she lost it entirely when her mother threw her out of the house, but she kept finding new ways to build up hope and then destroy it. Simultaneously naive and worldly, really.<br /><br />Loved it. From cover to cover.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11117499866905148272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who enjoyed The Dress Lodger or The Crimson Petal &amp; The WHite</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23071222" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15634098511988700907">Well, from all the books I've read about this time period, it has become alarmingly clear that a woman such as myself would not have existed back then.<br/><br/>Women were allowed only a few scripted roles, one of which was prostitute. For any woman who didn't find the confines of holy matrimony a sacrifice worth making, there was always the stree whore, the slammerkin, the dress lodger, the bar wench or the mistress. All were examples of the same thing; a woman who exchanged sexual relations for money an</span>
  <span id="freeText15634098511988700907" style="display:none">Well, from all the books I've read about this time period, it has become alarmingly clear that a woman such as myself would not have existed back then.<br /><br />Women were allowed only a few scripted roles, one of which was prostitute. For any woman who didn't find the confines of holy matrimony a sacrifice worth making, there was always the stree whore, the slammerkin, the dress lodger, the bar wench or the mistress. All were examples of the same thing; a woman who exchanged sexual relations for money and/or power (of a sort).<br /><br />Most woman who chose door #2 often made bad ends; the heroine in this novel (or is that anti-heroine?) is no different. Probably some long hold-over (hangover?) from morality tales of the past. Like the Hayes Code for movies, if you will. A woman who sells herself for money can't possibly end up well off and comfortable, because that would promote such a thing, right? Right??<br /><br />Other than that amount of predictability, this is a better-than-decent read. It flows pretty well, and our heroines adventures fly fairly high until the inevitable denouement.<br /><br />You do care about the characters, for the most part. Though, not deeply. The comeuppance she receives doesn't encite schaudenfreude, rather a terse shake of the head - tsk, tsk.<br /><br />Fans of historical novels, however, will likely enjoy it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15634098511988700907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_820247810" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/820247810" />
    <a title="Laura Leaney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3633494-laura-leaney"><img alt="Laura Leaney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592839426p2/3633494.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/820247810?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura Leaney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura Leaney" href="/user/show/3633494-laura-leaney">Laura Leaney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer820247810" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13896747791431297793">When I was just a young thing (when was that ever possible?) I had a near obsession with England, especially the age of Austen. It was the equipment and accoutrement of the rich that particularly fascinated me. Jane Austen probably began this, but there must have been some other source that I cannot recall. Even the poverty stricken orphans of the later Dickensian world were painted with Romance for me, because didn't some of them get found? Didn't the end turn out all right? Look at Oliver. Wha</span>
  <span id="freeText13896747791431297793" style="display:none">When I was just a young thing (when was that ever possible?) I had a near obsession with England, especially the age of Austen. It was the equipment and accoutrement of the rich that particularly fascinated me. Jane Austen probably began this, but there must have been some other source that I cannot recall. Even the poverty stricken orphans of the later Dickensian world were painted with Romance for me, because didn't some of them get found? Didn't the end turn out all right? Look at Oliver. Whatever blackness existed, I know I blocked it out. <br /><br />This book makes that blackness extremely real. <i>Slammerkin</i> is a brutal look at one particular facet of the Georgian era underclass: prostitution. Donoghue's writing is luscious, but she writes in the rich imagery of rot. Like fruit gone bad. London is an explosion of color and activity, carriage wheels and powdered wigs. Within the current of its streets are the men (cullies) rutting with girls against alley walls, pimps shining with sweat, the high colour of rouge and coralled lips, the clap, the stink of sex, and dead eyes like glass. It's horrible and fascinating. A bit like opening a privy door to 1760.<br /><br />The focus of the book is Mary Saunders, a courageous girl with a core of something distinctly unlikeable wedged inside her heart. Her journey begins with the craving for a bit of red ribbon. It takes her straight to hell. I didn't see too many choices for her on the way though.<br /><br />What I really like about the book is the author's brilliant use of period detail. Stomachers, corsets, improvers, stays.........everything is here. <br /><br />What I can't quite get over is the shallow nature of Mary Saunders. I should have felt more, but her character is too flat for my taste. Something seems missing in her - but perhaps that's the nature of psychopaths. Is she one? It's hard to tell. She is certainly hardened beyond belief, and if I'd understood more of her younger years I might have been able to glimpse the soul of her.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13896747791431297793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18005852400465913658">Been a long time since I read a book I really liked, couldnt wait to get back to and genuinely learned something from and I got all that from this one gem..It is a dark, chilling tale of young girl whose thirst for the finer things in life lead her to the dark side as a fallen woman--but hooray prostitutes, they are the strongest, fiercest and funniest characters in the book and Doll (she reminds me of Sugar from another great book on harlots The Crimson Petal and the White) is my favorite but I</span>
  <span id="freeText18005852400465913658" style="display:none">Been a long time since I read a book I really liked, couldnt wait to get back to and genuinely learned something from and I got all that from this one gem..It is a dark, chilling tale of young girl whose thirst for the finer things in life lead her to the dark side as a fallen woman--but hooray prostitutes, they are the strongest, fiercest and funniest characters in the book and Doll (she reminds me of Sugar from another great book on harlots The Crimson Petal and the White) is my favorite but I come to actually admire Mary and how silly she is..She realizes that her body is all she has and she decides to use it to get what she wants though the honest career of sewing she was made for is open and available to her. In this book I learned three great rules : Never give up your liberty--Clothes make the woman---and Clothes are the greatest lie ever told--and honestly I plan on using these rules and applying them in 2009...I enjoyed this book, felt a little cheated at the end but only because it felt too short, contrived and not hardly fitting of the girl you grow to like despite her flaws..Sassy and dark read but thoroughly enjoyable.</span>

<div id="review_2089323212" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2089323212" />
    <a title="Faye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56458845-faye"><img alt="Faye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522941281p2/56458845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2089323212?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Faye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Faye" href="/user/show/56458845-faye">Faye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56458845-faye?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56458845-faye?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2089323212" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8882367407432814580">Read: August 2017<br/>Rating: 4/5 stars<br/><br/>This is the third Emma Donoghue novel I've read (the first being <i>The Sealed Letter</i> and the second being <i>Room</i>) and based on the strength of all three, Donoghue is rapidly becoming one of my 'auto-buy authors.'<br/><br/>I definitely benefited from the fact that I knew absolutely nothing about this book when I started reading <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[to the point I didn't even know Mary Saunders was a real person <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and I would urge anyone who wants to read this to try an</span>
  <span id="freeText8882367407432814580" style="display:none">Read: August 2017<br />Rating: 4/5 stars<br /><br />This is the third Emma Donoghue novel I've read (the first being <i>The Sealed Letter</i> and the second being <i>Room</i>) and based on the strength of all three, Donoghue is rapidly becoming one of my 'auto-buy authors.'<br /><br />I definitely benefited from the fact that I knew absolutely nothing about this book when I started reading <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[to the point I didn't even know Mary Saunders was a real person <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and I would urge anyone who wants to read this to try and remain spoiler free themselves.<br /><br />What I liked:<br />- The descriptions of both London and Monmouth in the late 1700s felt very vibrant and real.<br />- I liked Mary as a character; I know a lot of other reviewers didn't like her but I didn't feel like she had to be 'nice' in order for me to empathise with her. I liked her sharpness, her ambition, her resourcefulness, and I felt sorry for all the things she had gone through in her young life, which destroyed any faith or trust she had in other people, especially in men.<br />- The female characterisation all around was excellent; Doll, Jane Jones, Abi, and Mrs Ash were all well rounded and nuanced characters that didn't just fulfil one role; they were all a mixture of good and bad, weak and strong, and making right and wrong decisions which affected the way in which the story unfolded.<br />-<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[That shock ending totally took me by surprise! I had been rooting for Mary throughout the book, but Donoghue masterfully showed us how Mary gradually reached breaking point and turned on the one person who loved her and would do anything for her. It was heartbreaking to see how Mary always had this chance to turn her life around; she was so talented as a seamstress, she could've gone into business with Jane and made the success of herself that she always wanted to be, but in the end her own impatience and self-destructiveness destroyed it all. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />What I didn't like:<br />- Mary's mother was very quick to make her only daughter homeless at such a young age, and I don't think that was very realistic and happened only to advance the plot.<br />- While the female characterisation was wonderfully subtle and realistic, the men never really got the same treatment. Aside from Mary's dead father who always seemed like a heroic figure, the other men were all weak and one dimensional; <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[even Mr Jones, who at first seemed to escape this stock 'men are bad' categorisation ended up being one of the worst, cheating on his wife, having sex with Mary, and ordering his wife to beat her <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8882367407432814580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_807663393" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/807663393" />
    <a title="Suzanne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne"><img alt="Suzanne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1369500256p2/3695758.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/807663393?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Suzanne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne" href="/user/show/3695758-suzanne">Suzanne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of historical fiction</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Suzanne by: </span>
      $1 at library sale
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3695758-suzanne?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer807663393" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer854927148196459154"><b>“Clothes outlived people, she knew that. Clothes were more of a sure thing.”</b><br/><br/>“Slammerkin” refers to both a loose woman and a loose gown, and the sartorial is a constant motif in this story about a teen-aged prostitute named Mary Saunders in London and Monmouth, a Welsh border town, in the 1760s. There was an actual person of this name, but Emma Donoghue had only a very few sketchy facts from which she created this moving fiction that effectively illustrates that, in the mid-18th century anyway, l</span>
  <span id="freeText854927148196459154" style="display:none"><b>“Clothes outlived people, she knew that. Clothes were more of a sure thing.”</b><br /><br />“Slammerkin” refers to both a loose woman and a loose gown, and the sartorial is a constant motif in this story about a teen-aged prostitute named Mary Saunders in London and Monmouth, a Welsh border town, in the 1760s. There was an actual person of this name, but Emma Donoghue had only a very few sketchy facts from which she created this moving fiction that effectively illustrates that, in the mid-18th century anyway, life was short and brutal, and then you died. The journey was especially harsh if you didn’t have any money, and for women, it was even more harrowing due to the paucity of options. <br /> <br />Donoghue’s Mary has a grim-faced mother who works as a low-paid sewer of piece work, the bottom of the seamstress barrel. Drabness and drudgery are something the 14-year-old Mary abhors and she’s sure she’s meant for finer things. She wants color in her life, freedom from the work that’s making her mother prematurely old, and a future that is better than the dreary existence she sees all around her. Dream on, little Mary, dream on. <br /><br />Her path is chosen by fate when she becomes covetous of a pretty red ribbon, is molested by the ribbon vendor, becomes pregnant, and is thrown out on the streets by her hard-working, pious mother. After a gang rape leaves her close to death, she is taken in by a local whore who shelters her and trains in the tricks of the trade. Despite the hardships of this life, she still finds it suits her better than the more respectable but constricting, back-breaking, and soul-crushing alternatives open to her. It allows her to attain her most important desire, which is her liberty. She also learns that, out in the world, “clothes make the woman” and that “clothes are the greatest lie ever told.” Her appearance is a means to getting what she wants. Not only does she want to look pretty – what girl doesn’t?—but there is a practical aspect too. It keeps her from falling even further socially and economically, into the kind of destitution that means rapid death by starvation in a freezing alley or being murdered by someone even more desperate. And her realization that appearances can be deceiving reinforces her belief that the better-dressed are not necessarily superior to her, further strengthening her desire for social mobility. <br /><br />There is much description throughout the book of clothing, fabrics, and women’s accessories which are integral to the story, but that might be off-putting to some readers (read: “men”). But this emphasis on apparel and fashion all ties in with Mary’s need for some beauty in her world and with her conviction that a certain appearance is a survival technique.<br /><br />Concern about survival is at the core of Mary’s motivations and her capacity for caring about others or her actions’ effects on them is sorely limited, understandable given her youth and her small experience with anything resembling trusting or nurturing relationships. Nor is she is temperamentally suited to the resignation displayed by so many working class types she has contact with, such as her mother, or a mother-substitute later in the story when she takes temporary shelter at the home of a family friend. Their philosophy is, where you are is where you’re supposed to be; it doesn’t occur to them to question or try to change their lot. They keep their heads down and spend their grueling days being grateful it isn’t even worse. At her mother’s old friend’s home Mary manages to fake a life as a respectable servant girl / seamstress apprentice for a while, learning more about sewing and fashion that she hopes to use to elevate herself later, daydreaming, as girls will, about a glamorous life. Getting an education about better quality clothing stokes her desires for even finer things, without getter her any closer to a real means of attaining them. <br /><br />Mary yearns for freedom, class mobility and beauty. For life to be nothing but servitude, under the control of other for bare sustenance, and daily bleakness without hope is not something her young and spirited but rather cold heart can ever come to terms with. And in that time and place, this drive rebounds on her to a world of hurt. Mary’s narcissistic character might not be an admirable one, but I did feel some compassion for this girl, slightly more than a child, who hardly stood a chance in these circumstances, and who had few opportunities to develop close emotional connections or a moral compass. There was a point where a couple of tenuous threads connecting her with others began to be spun, but in the end they are too thin to withstand the events that befall her and her own weaknesses, ambition, and restlessness. Mary at 14 and 15 doesn’t have the maturity to develop any view other than that which centers on her immediate wants and needs and fears. It is not a happy story, but a well-told one. <br /><br />In spite of the potential for this to be a depressing story, I did not find it so, although parts of it were sad. Donoghue manages to weave a compelling tale about distressing situations in an engaging way, just as she did in <i>Room</i>. The details of this world are colorful and vividly drawn, from the symptoms of the diseases the whores were prone to, to the sumptuous embroidered fabrics worn by the well-to-do, to the customs at seasonal festivals and funerals, to all the family fun to be had at public executions, with their picnicking and rope-bit souvenirs. <br /><br />Recommended for fans of historical fiction. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="854927148196459154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jen from Quebec :0)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4911921-jen-from-quebec-0"><img alt="Jen from Quebec :0)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488084323p2/4911921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1924953845?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen from Quebec :0)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen from Quebec :0)" href="/user/show/4911921-jen-from-quebec-0">Jen from Quebec :0)</a>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781554684700" href="/book/show/6622793-slammerkin">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1924953845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12799859850723412260">Wow- This was a fantastic novel, and full of surprises. It is now among my favorites. <br/><br/>Emma Donoghue really does her homework, bringing the London of the 1750's alive with an astonishing amount of details. The description on the book jacket leads readers to believe that this book is simply about CLOTHES and a profound love of clothing that the main character, Mary, has. And, while a large portion of the book *is* indeed centered around the importance of clothing and the various 'costumes' that pe</span>
  <span id="freeText12799859850723412260" style="display:none">Wow- This was a fantastic novel, and full of surprises. It is now among my favorites. <br /><br />Emma Donoghue really does her homework, bringing the London of the 1750's alive with an astonishing amount of details. The description on the book jacket leads readers to believe that this book is simply about CLOTHES and a profound love of clothing that the main character, Mary, has. And, while a large portion of the book *is* indeed centered around the importance of clothing and the various 'costumes' that people wear, it is actually (thankfully) about SO much MORE. <br /><br />At the age of 14, Mary is raped by a Ribbon Seller, after seeing a red ribbon in the hair of a street prostitute and wanting such a fine ribbon for herself. (Yes- the rapist was a Ribbon Seller- an actual profession in 18th century London). Despite being the victim, she is kicked out of her home for 'losing her virtue' with the rape and has to turn to a life of street prostitution at the age of 15 in order to survive. Here, we meet Doll Higgins, a fellow harlot and Mary's new best friend- ironically, she is the whore that owned that ribbon which started all of Mary's angst. <br /><br />What follows is a detailed story about life on the streets, and Mary's struggles to get by in a depraved area of London. Eventually, she moves to a small town and becomes a servant to a dressmaker, surrounding herself with the clothing she has always loved. I will not tell you the rest, as it is best to read the twists and turns for yourself. However, I assure you-this story never goes in the direction that you think it will. And I have (perhaps) never met a protagonist that I felt more sorry for than for poor Mary Saunders in 'Slammerkin'. <br /><br />Highly recommended - a brilliant work of historical fiction. The book cover's synopsis does not at all do justice to the astute story contained within these pages.--Jen from Quebec :0)</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3151047460" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3151047460" />
    <a title="Heather" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10738912-heather"><img alt="Heather" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587571845p2/10738912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3151047460?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heather" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heather" href="/user/show/10738912-heather">Heather</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781554684700" href="/book/show/6622793-slammerkin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3151047460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7793591464333198668">"It came to Daffy then, how easily the worst in oneself could rise up and strike a blow. How even the most enlightened man had little power over his own darkness."<br/><br/>Once again, Emma Donoghue has taken a tiny snippet from "The Gentleman's Magazine" and a broadsheet entitled "The Confession and Last Dying-Words of Mary Saunders", added in some real-life characters, invented some others, and imagined a story. Not a feel-good story. Not a happily ever after story. A well-written, insightful story abou</span>
  <span id="freeText7793591464333198668" style="display:none">"It came to Daffy then, how easily the worst in oneself could rise up and strike a blow. How even the most enlightened man had little power over his own darkness."<br /><br />Once again, Emma Donoghue has taken a tiny snippet from "The Gentleman's Magazine" and a broadsheet entitled "The Confession and Last Dying-Words of Mary Saunders", added in some real-life characters, invented some others, and imagined a story. Not a feel-good story. Not a happily ever after story. A well-written, insightful story about a character I haven't met before. The above named thirteen-year-old, pragmatic but naïve, Mary.<br /><br />When we meet Mary, she is an innocent girl living in dire poverty in Cheapside, London, with her mother, stepfather, and baby brother in the late 1700s. Mary longs for all the things every teenager wants: freedom, fun, excitement, friendship, money, beautiful clothes -- all the superficial wants. Although intelligent, surprisingly well educated, and (we find out) talented, Mary never thinks much deeper than this throughout the story, but why would she? What teenager does? Throughout my reading, I kept Mary's age at the forefront of my mind. I thought it was key to her character. I also kept thinking about the time --the 1700s. Did girls like Mary go to school? She was definitely there for a much longer time than I would have thought. I didn't think there even was a childhood back then, and definitely there was no teenage-hood. Not for the poor and hungry. There were no safety nets in London in those days. There is no welfare. There are workhouses and poorhouses. And there is the oldest profession in the world. <br /><br />Without giving away the story, Mary's childhood very quickly transforms from a life of simple suffering (not enough to eat, somewhat neglected, unloved because you are a girl and there is a baby brother to love, a little mistrusted, and very much unwanted) to one of having to make extreme, adult choices. She wants more than what her parents have, (bare subsistence), but a homeless teenager (and female to boot) really has very limited options living in an uncaring city. You survive through your own means or you don't. It is noted many times over the course of the story how little worth the female has. Mary dared to hope to rise above her station. Whether or not that could have happened, we will never know.<br /><br />While not exactly liking Mary, I could understand her desires. We all wish for something better. I thought she was pragmatic and a survivor. She is living in a time and in a world that I will never know. Like her friend Doll tells her early on, "It's every girl for herself, you understand?" She is the product of a short but hard life. Emma Donoghue, making me think the deep thoughts 🤗 </span>
  <a data-text-id="7793591464333198668" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_175464787" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/175464787" />
    <a title="Diane " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/143168-diane"><img alt="Diane " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558395550p2/143168.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/175464787?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane " href="/user/show/143168-diane">Diane </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780547443294" href="/book/show/10114261-slammerkin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer175464787" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11246892132480676056">Must start out by saying this is the second book I've read by Emma Donoghue, and I think she is a brilliant writer. <br/><br/>Slammerkin was one of the darkest and saddest books I have read. The book covers 3 years in the life of Mary Saunders, and takes place in the mid 1700's in London and, primarily, Monmouth. It should also be noted that according the author's note, it is based on a real person. <br/><br/>Mary's home in London is one of poverty; when she finds herself pregnant and disowned by her mother and ste</span>
  <span id="freeText11246892132480676056" style="display:none">Must start out by saying this is the second book I've read by Emma Donoghue, and I think she is a brilliant writer. <br /><br />Slammerkin was one of the darkest and saddest books I have read. The book covers 3 years in the life of Mary Saunders, and takes place in the mid 1700's in London and, primarily, Monmouth. It should also be noted that according the author's note, it is based on a real person. <br /><br />Mary's home in London is one of poverty; when she finds herself pregnant and disowned by her mother and step-father early on in the story, she turns to a life of prostitution where she finds she can make money. Mary is obsessed with money and finery, determined not to live the life her mother leads. She gets into serious trouble and is hunted by a pimp which leads her to Monmouth to escape him; there her plan is to meet her mother's best friend, Mrs. Jones, and concocts a lie so that Mrs. Jones takes her into her family.<br /><br />What's saddest about this book in my opinion is that Mary did love the life she had with Mrs. Jones and her family; it was the first time she really had a sense of family. BUT the story and Mary unravel.....and the story turns to one of despair and crushing sadness. <br /><br />The reason for my 3 star rating is because I think the story could have been told just as effectively in a shorter version; it encompassed only 3 years, yet it was close to 400 pages. It would have been a 4 star for me easily if the story were edited down. Also, some of the sex scenes were described way too graphic for my tastes, a couple of them made my skin crawl. <br /><br />Some classic lines of prose in here:<br /><br />"Grief was a pricey business."<br />"For all the books in his possesssion, he still failed to read the stories written plain as day in the faces of the people around him."<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11246892132480676056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_172248201" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172248201" />
    <a title="Doug Bradshaw" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/808994-doug-bradshaw"><img alt="Doug Bradshaw" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548429066p2/808994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172248201?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Doug Bradshaw" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Doug Bradshaw" href="/user/show/808994-doug-bradshaw">Doug Bradshaw</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer172248201&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172248201" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12711937996287452004">I had to read the Goodreads meaning of each of the stars. One star means "didn't like it." If there were different categories of ratings such as "rate the author's writing skill" I could have given it a four or a five. She is an excellent writer and there was a lot of excellent research and history incorporated into the story which we find on the last page was loosely based on a real incident. It was a Dickensian story but without any redemption. One of my favorite words is lugubrious and look f</span>
  <span id="freeText12711937996287452004" style="display:none">I had to read the Goodreads meaning of each of the stars. One star means "didn't like it." If there were different categories of ratings such as "rate the author's writing skill" I could have given it a four or a five. She is an excellent writer and there was a lot of excellent research and history incorporated into the story which we find on the last page was loosely based on a real incident. It was a Dickensian story but without any redemption. One of my favorite words is lugubrious and look for places to use it. This is truly a lugubrious tale and I have to wonder if the story needed to be told. Wouldn't it be much better to tell the story of a young abused child of 14 years pushed out into the streets and treated kindly by a prostitute and then who becomes a prostitute to make ends meet who then finds her way out of that pit and becomes successful? But this isn't that story. <br /><br />I'm not going to tell the story except to say, there isn't one truly good thing that happens to any character in the book and almost every person in the book was somewhat messed up. There were no happy or good things going on ever in the book and every character had some kind of evil or misguided by religious thinking motive behind their actions. The somewhat likable "Doll" the older hooker who took Mary in may be a bit of an exception to this and I do think she had sex for fun sometimes, but that's about the only slightly fun thing or good thing I can think of in the book. <br /><br />I'm not a prude like, say Erin, but I also found the whole first section of the book to be stinky, ugly, creepy and yucky (I'm using PG13 descriptions unlike the book) with a 14 year old girl having various sexual encounters with creepy men. I wish they'd've edited her in at an older age and that there were at least one or two decent guys, maybe handsome sailors or something, that were good to her. <br /><br />Read this book if you want a depressing and sad story of an abused and unloved young girl who had a crappy existence. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12711937996287452004" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_72498973" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72498973" />
    <a title="Tara Chevrestt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2544368-tara-chevrestt"><img alt="Tara Chevrestt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1399003936p2/2544368.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72498973?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara Chevrestt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara Chevrestt" href="/user/show/2544368-tara-chevrestt">Tara Chevrestt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2544368-tara-chevrestt?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2544368-tara-chevrestt?shelf=england">england</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72498973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17726517311690479791">I read the whole thing, to my shock. In the author's defense, there was a vast and rather intriguing group of characters, but none of them were likeable. There is the bitter mother that rues the day she gave birth to a worthless girl, prostitute whose life was utterly pointless, the tailor lady that thought it ok to have a slave, the minister that was also a pimp on the side, the disloyal husband, the religious but hateful fanatic, and last, but certainly not least, an incredible spoiled brat, w</span>
  <span id="freeText17726517311690479791" style="display:none">I read the whole thing, to my shock. In the author's defense, there was a vast and rather intriguing group of characters, but none of them were likeable. There is the bitter mother that rues the day she gave birth to a worthless girl, prostitute whose life was utterly pointless, the tailor lady that thought it ok to have a slave, the minister that was also a pimp on the side, the disloyal husband, the religious but hateful fanatic, and last, but certainly not least, an incredible spoiled brat, which is the heroine in this story. The closest I came to feeling sorry or feeling anything at all for any of these characters was the slave. Every other character in this book is a hypocrit. The jolly prostitute may be the only exception. She at least was honest about what she was and held her up and drank and was cheerful. She did not feel the need to kill, steal, or hurt others to rise above her "station" in life. All in all, this book disgusted me. There was a little too much detail about the "cullies" (male customers) and the actual acts. However, it was interesting to get a look at the non royal life. I have read so much about the rich royal family, that it was interesting to get a feel for what went on outside the palaces in that time. Thus, I gave it two stars. However, this book is not memorable and will not enrich your life in any way. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17726517311690479791" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/64624702?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karla" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karla" href="/user/show/2428829-karla">Karla</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer64624702" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer602531929889374265">This is irresistible transporting fiction. Emma Donoghhue writes a a story inspired by few surviving facts of the real Mary Saunder's life. At 14 she is thrust into the London streets by her mother and thought of as worthless trash. Mary meets so many bawdy characters like Dall one tuff talking street guide, who teaches her how to make money in the oldest profession known for survival. Mary's desire of vanity holds no bounds although she is given many chances for a fresh life she only knows one </span>
  <span id="freeText602531929889374265" style="display:none">This is irresistible transporting fiction. Emma Donoghhue writes a a story inspired by few surviving facts of the real Mary Saunder's life. At 14 she is thrust into the London streets by her mother and thought of as worthless trash. Mary meets so many bawdy characters like Dall one tuff talking street guide, who teaches her how to make money in the oldest profession known for survival. Mary's desire of vanity holds no bounds although she is given many chances for a fresh life she only knows one way to get what her heart desires, the luxury of fine clothes and fame. This is a book I know made me think deeply of today's lost youth who don't know how to receive love cause their heart has hardened and can't absorb it. Mary only saw the evil in others and that seemed to be her downward spiral. Some can't change the only way they know to live cause it's the only way for them. I loved this book and look forward to much more of Donoghue's intelligent and absorbing writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="602531929889374265" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_89778139" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89778139" />
    <a title="Juushika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616321-juushika"><img alt="Juushika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541586729p2/616321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89778139?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juushika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juushika" href="/user/show/616321-juushika">Juushika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=status-owned">status-owned</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89778139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11991413616639090873">1760s London, and Mary Saunders desires more than the meager life of her widowed mother. Her lust for ribbon, lace, and the fineries of status leads her to sell the only thing she has: her body. Donoghue is a skillful writer, and Mary's journey as a London prostitute and a country maid is gritty, dark, and depressing, but driven by Mary's vivid personality. Multiple points of view in the second half of the novel detract from Mary's story, but on the whole this is a dark but burning novel which r</span>
  <span id="freeText11991413616639090873" style="display:none">1760s London, and Mary Saunders desires more than the meager life of her widowed mother. Her lust for ribbon, lace, and the fineries of status leads her to sell the only thing she has: her body. Donoghue is a skillful writer, and Mary's journey as a London prostitute and a country maid is gritty, dark, and depressing, but driven by Mary's vivid personality. Multiple points of view in the second half of the novel detract from Mary's story, but on the whole this is a dark but burning novel which raises thoughtful issues of family, gender roles, and liberty. I recommend it.<br /><br /><i>Slammerkin</i> a dense, intelligent, skillfully crafted novel. Donoghue does so much right that it feels excessive to list it all: Mary is willful, faulted, and startlingly real, the perfect protagonist to draw a reader deep into her story. Donoghue's writing is tight but descriptive, a combination which keeps direction through gritty descriptions of London and thoughtful exploration of the novel's themes. And these themes are abundant: with her heart set on liberty in a culture that offers little, Mary comes head to head with issues of gender roles and social prejudice, of familial love, of economic and personal freedom. Donoghue is intelligent and never preachy: she approaches her themes obliquely or couches them in desire, and they are personal and real. But for all that the novel does well, whether it's enjoyable is a different question. It's deeply settled within 1760s Englanda gritty, dirty, disease-ridden, prejudiced, poor, distinctly unpleasant place which glamorizes nothing. <i>Slammerkin</i> is often dark and depressing, and it not a pleasure read.<br /><br />Instead, the novel should be read for Mary's journeywhich is not joyful, but is driven. The first half of <i>Slammerkin</i> closely follows her point of view, and her journey is as horrific as it is admirable: though never glamorous, her life of prostitution is sometimes joyful. The second half branches out into multiple points of view, which better conveys the plot of Mary's horrible endbut distracts from the personal fire that takes Mary on her journey. In her lust for liberty and luxury, Mary falls somewhere between brave and brash, between inspired and greedy. She is imperfect, but she is undeniably real. In the first half of the book, her strength of character pushes her forward. But multiple POVs distract the reader from Mary's character, and the book suffers. It's still good, and the ending is terrible and beautiful, but the book would be bettermore impassioned, and more enjoyable despite the dark settingif it closer followed Mary's story through to the end. I recommend <i>Slammerkin</i>despite this weakness, it's a skillful and intelligent book of fire burning in the dark: a brave battle against the worst possible odds.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11991413616639090873" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Annaleise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7995271-annaleise"><img alt="Annaleise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507276908p2/7995271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/680827223?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annaleise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annaleise" href="/user/show/7995271-annaleise">Annaleise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781844087341" href="/book/show/10625954-slammerkin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7995271-annaleise?shelf=deserves-more-stars">deserves-more-stars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer680827223" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4866642630200011078">"Slammerkin: a loose gown, a loose woman". <br/><br/>I love the concept behind Slammerkin; Donoghue has based her novel on fragments of an old news story from 1744, about Mary Suanders, a 16 year old girl who reportedly died because she had a desire for fine clothes.<br/><br/>Mary isn't a particularly sympathetic character; at times I wondered if she was perhaps a little mad. But given the circumstances she lives through, it's understandable if she became a bit unhinged. She's a surprisingly believable and satisfyi</span>
  <span id="freeText4866642630200011078" style="display:none">"Slammerkin: a loose gown, a loose woman". <br /><br />I love the concept behind Slammerkin; Donoghue has based her novel on fragments of an old news story from 1744, about Mary Suanders, a 16 year old girl who reportedly died because she had a desire for fine clothes.<br /><br />Mary isn't a particularly sympathetic character; at times I wondered if she was perhaps a little mad. But given the circumstances she lives through, it's understandable if she became a bit unhinged. She's a surprisingly believable and satisfying, if not likable, heroine. <br /><br />The quality of the writing was excellent, though it's hard to believe Slammerkin and Room came from the same author; they are so different. The thing they have in common is that both novels make your skin crawl in an uncomfortable way, but at the same time, Slammerkin holds your attention right to the end. I struggled to put it down and I was fascinated by the details, even if they were disgusting at times!<br /><br /><b>Good bits</b>: Donoghue's creativity in weaving Slammerkin out of off-cuts from another, real life story, and the obvious effort that has gone in to the historical details of daily life in the 18th C. <br /><b>Bad Bits</b>: not really a criticism, but I'd recommend NOT reading this with food. <br /><b>Read this with</b>: If you like historical fiction, and you don't shy away from a bit of gore for the sake of an excellent plot - this is for you. <br /><br />NB: While I enjoyed reading Room, Slammerkin is a whole other thing. Very different, and in my humble opinion, a better read. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_136110931" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136110931" />
    <a title="Cat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78792-cat"><img alt="Cat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199044332p2/78792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/136110931?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cat" href="/user/show/78792-cat">Cat</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer136110931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1442461967818314336">Such a compelling, satisfying read! Donoghue's narrative voice is witty, idiomatic, evocative, and compelling. The psychologies of all of the characters (even some of the ones who could easily be marginal and stereotyped, like the former wet-nurse Mrs. Ash) are finely and convincingly drawn. The book has a rollicking pace without sacrificing a profound sense of the misery of human suffering and female embodiment (within prostitution, slavery, and poverty). Donoghue slyly inserts into Mary Saunde</span>
  <span id="freeText1442461967818314336" style="display:none">Such a compelling, satisfying read! Donoghue's narrative voice is witty, idiomatic, evocative, and compelling. The psychologies of all of the characters (even some of the ones who could easily be marginal and stereotyped, like the former wet-nurse Mrs. Ash) are finely and convincingly drawn. The book has a rollicking pace without sacrificing a profound sense of the misery of human suffering and female embodiment (within prostitution, slavery, and poverty). Donoghue slyly inserts into Mary Saunders' tale a canny historical awareness of the shift into a capitalist culture and the veneer of Enlightenment that disguises something much darker about the birth of modernity. Mary's desire for something finer (attached to the finery of the nobility) and her aspiration to play a part both say something about the rise of commodity culture and the instability of identity that it entails and promises. Donoghue demonstrates that social hierarchies are not so easy to scale and that the fall is perilous, especially for a fallen woman. She does this without losing compassion or humor and without blunting the edge of her book with either of those often softening qualities. I poured through this book with total engagement. The historical details never swamp the well-plotted story, and the tragic tone and finale do not drown the spark of the defiant (anti)heroine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1442461967818314336" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
