<div id="review_271509278" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271509278" />
    <a title="Aubrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey"><img alt="Aubrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472535045p2/4119460.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271509278?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aubrey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aubrey" href="/user/show/4119460-aubrey">Aubrey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reality-check">reality-check</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=queer-as-in">queer-as-in</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=2-star">2-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-2019">r-2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=1-read-on-hand">1-read-on-hand</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=r-goodreads">r-goodreads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=virago">virago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-all">antidote-think-twice-all</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=antidote-think-twice-read">antidote-think-twice-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4119460-aubrey?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271509278" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1776641818796287784">For all this book's reasonably substantial length, there's not much to it. It won a specifically named lesbian award without having any specific queer content (which is why I mistakenly picked it up during Queer History Month in the first place), glossed over an otherwise fascinating period of history that was much less white and straight than what common consensus proclaims, and otherwise went through the motions of narrating a story without ever rising above the bare minimum. I'm giving one st</span>
  <span id="freeText1776641818796287784" style="display:none">For all this book's reasonably substantial length, there's not much to it. It won a specifically named lesbian award without having any specific queer content (which is why I mistakenly picked it up during Queer History Month in the first place), glossed over an otherwise fascinating period of history that was much less white and straight than what common consensus proclaims, and otherwise went through the motions of narrating a story without ever rising above the bare minimum. I'm giving one star for the obvious research and another for the good intentions of the author in fleshing out a story of a sixteen year old girl <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[condemned to death for unknown reasons <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Unfortunately, very little of this long ago person's story truly drew me in, whether it was set in the garish vitality of London or the semi-colonized bleakness of Monmouth. This was especially disappointing in a work of historical fiction, which is a genre that I can't seem to help relying on when it comes to extending my learning regarding far off climes and chronologies in, ideally, as uniquely phrased and/or sensually prosed and/or certain je ne sais quoi a mode as possible. This, for a variety of reasons, just didn't make the cut.<br /><br />The most interest I had in this book was whenever the main character drew towards a realization of the ubiquity of capitalist exploitation, whether body or mind. Beyond that, I got a lot of surface level dictation of entrenched misogyny, racism, poverty, and sex work, admittedly with more realistically portrayed diversity than I'm used to, but nothing of especial narrative note, whether theme or prose. It felt like a certain strength of narrative was lost when the setting dramatically shifted, and the new multi-person point of view that accompanied such made it feel as if the story had taken 140 pages to truly get started, except it didn't so much start as stagnated. I acknowledge the character development that grew out of the shift, but the types of development described were all old topics that've been gone over many a time, and the added complications of male heir obsession, patriarchal disability, embroidery, and even the sexual selling of one's body never did much to make this narrative shine. All in all, another historical piece of fiction that came across a random tidbit and attempted to expand upon it with the idea that, either it's never been done before, or they were going to do it in so singularly well a fashion that it would rise above the crowd. You'd think nearly 400 pages would have been enough to achieve that much, but alas.<br /><br />My obsession with getting rid of the longest lasting residents of my shelves continues, and in the wake of getting a record ten (!) works already on the TBR at a bonus sale of the month (in addition to another five brand new ones for largely Quest for Women purpose), it's vital for my storage space to clear out room for the new. Oftentimes, that means coming to authors long after the initial point of interest had passed, and when it comes to Donoghue, all I can say is that I'm glad I gave up on the hype of her 'Room' before I actually took the plunge, as now I have no more works of hers to worry about. The description of this conjured up images of Carter-level phantasmagoric eroticism, and what I got was a rather straightforward tale of ruin and deprivation: never quite noble enough to inspire, never sufficiently grotesque to fascinate. All the components were in this one, but this novel's strength did not lie in the multi layering, and the moment it took on that effort was the moment it began to fizzle out. As such, a dismally low ratio of oomph to page count, and yet another work I'll bear in mind when next I take a change on historical fiction.<blockquote>
  <i>Masters and mistresses were only cullies by another name, according to Nan. You pretended to be satisfied, or grateful, even. You served them, but they never knew you. You robbed them of whatever you could, because whatever they paid, it was never enough for what they asked.</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="1776641818796287784" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1370251755" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1370251755" />
    <a title="Kaitlin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin"><img alt="Kaitlin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481663259p2/19126246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1370251755?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaitlin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaitlin" href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin">Kaitlin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781844088201" href="/book/show/15956107-slammerkin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1370251755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17351319082693911212">Okay, so this is the first Donoghue book I have read and it's a book which I knew very little about before reading or buying it as I picked this up mainly because I loved the cover and there were a few people I knew planning to do a buddy read of it and I wanted to join them. A few things I know now as I've finished it up, this is a historical fiction coming of age story where we follow Mary Saunders, a young girl who lives in London in the 1700s with her mother (a seamstress) and stepfather (he</span>
  <span id="freeText17351319082693911212" style="display:none">Okay, so this is the first Donoghue book I have read and it's a book which I knew very little about before reading or buying it as I picked this up mainly because I loved the cover and there were a few people I knew planning to do a buddy read of it and I wanted to join them. A few things I know now as I've finished it up, this is a historical fiction coming of age story where we follow Mary Saunders, a young girl who lives in London in the 1700s with her mother (a seamstress) and stepfather (her's died when she was barely born).<br /><br />I haven't read a great deal of Historical Fiction but it is always a genre which I quite enjoy because I have a fair fascination with Historical periods I don't know much about and always finding out more about them is interesting to me. Some things I particularly liked about this book were the setting, England (as I live in England and visit London a lot so I could recognise and picture some scenes a lot better than had it been set in New York for example). I also felt like Donoghue did a good job with setting the scene and making you feel like you could see it before you, not in a lyrical way, but more so a 'rough and ready' way.<br /><br />The main character of the story, Mary, is a young girl who has intelligence and wit, but gets herself into some rather sticky situations due to her vanity and love for fine things. She spies a beautiful ribbon or item of clothing and it's all she can think about, yet at the same time she knows what is a 'good' or 'respectable' thing to do (whether she does it or not is a whole other question). Mary is a character who I think it's easy to like. She's got some life in her and a good sense of adventure and fun, yet she's not necessarily sure of who or what she wants to be in her life. This leads her gradually further and further from her families plans for her, and sets her on some rather dark paths.<br /><br />The writing was easy to read, nothing overly fancy, but nothing too dull wither. We got simple descriptions of places and people, and Mary built relationships up as she went. We did get some quotes from both riddles, nursery rhymes and bible passages and these all helped to authenticate the time period and story and gave it a bit more of a whimsical, dark quality at times. <br /><br />On the whole I think it's a fairly fast and interesting historical fiction with some exciting events I didn't anticipate and some moments where I very much enjoyed it. I would say that if you haven't read much Historical Fiction then this is probably a great start point for its readability, and if you have any interest in the 'poorer' point of view for this time period then it's a quick read with some highlight moments. I gave this a 3.5*s as I liked it! :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17351319082693911212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Already there have been five mass extinctions in the last 600 million years, including the Cretaceous Extinction, during which an asteroid knocked out the dinosaurs. Though these events were initially destructive, they were also prime movers of evolutionary change in nature. And we can see some of the warning signs of another extinction event coming, as our oceans lose both fish and oxygen. In The Next Species, Michael Tennesen questions what life might be like after it happens.Tennesen discusses the future of nature and whether humans will make it through the bottleneck of extinction. Without man, could the seas regenerate to what they were before fishing vessels? Could life suddenly get very big as it did before the arrival of humans? And what if man survives the coming catastrophes, but in reduced populations? Would those groups be isolated enough to become distinct species? Could the conquest of Mars lead to another form of human? Could we upload our minds into a computer and live in a virtual reality? Or could genetic engineering create a more intelligent and long-lived creature that might shun the rest of us? And how would we recognize the next humans? Are they with us now?Tennesen delves into the history of the planet and travels to rainforests, canyons, craters, and caves all over the world to explore the potential winners and losers of the next era of evolution.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_54804820'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54804820-bluebird-memories?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Bluebird Memories: A Journey Through Lyrics &amp; Life<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/234808.Common?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Common<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.75 avg rating &mdash; 566 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published \n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer16992737299496532311\">“I’m an instrument, stay in tune with me.”\n\nIn a performance that combines the personal and poetic with the literary and the lyrical, Grammy, Oscar, Emmy, and Golden Globe-winning artist Common spins something entirely new and bold for both his old-s<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16992737299496532311\" style=\"display:none\">“I’m an instrument, stay in tune with me.”\n\nIn a performance that combines the personal and poetic with the literary and the lyrical, Grammy, Oscar, Emmy, and Golden Globe-winning artist Common spins something entirely new and bold for both his old-school fans and those new to one of hip-hop’s most essential voices. This is Bluebird Memories, part of Audible\'s Words + Music that blends storytelling, music, and performance to create a one-of-a-kind listening experience.\n\nWritten and conceived by Common with Awoye Timpo and NSangou Njikam, originally recorded live at the Minetta Lane Theatre in New York City, and backed by a world-class band including special guest appearance by Robert Glasper and an ensemble of vocalists, Bluebird Memories tracks Common’s intimate journey from childhood to manhood. At its heart, it serves as both an open letter to his estranged father and a personal meditation on the quest for greater self-understanding, self-worth, and connection to the world that surrounds him.\n\nAs a young artist, introduced to the work of literary icons from James Baldwin and Gwendolyn Brooks to Rakim and Melle Mel, Common would grow to use their works as motivation and a high-water mark for what would become his life in words. In a performance that recounts stories about growing up in Chicago, discovering hip-hop, longing for a consistent father, and his awakening into Black consciousness, the evolution of Common, as son, as man, artist, and activist, is all here - unpacked and laid bare.\n\nAt just under two hours, Bluebird Memories is constantly in motion, soaring with emotion and pulsing with raw vitality. Seamlessly blending, stretching, and playing with forms, Common slides between poetry and candid conversation, kicks into one of his classic tracks, and backs out into a moment of nearly silent reflection. For those hip-hop heads, the treat of hearing Common bounce between bars of his own self-admitted cringey party raps to rapturously reciting classic hip-hop’s most revered verses is a joy to behold. (Yes, bumping human beatbox included.)
